    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="cf1d70db612f417bb91af664ad4b1edc"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="cf1d70db612f417bb91af664ad4b1edc">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '7c2f956b-20bc-4b21-a1e4-6358b73b8ad8'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {

                x.parentNode.insertBefore(s, x);

   <div class="abstract-group  metis-abstract">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d90187498" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have gradually become one of the most promising energy storage techniques in the current era of post-lithium-ion batteries. For anodes, transitional metal selenides (TMSe) based materials are welcomed choices , owing to relatively higher specific capacities and enriched redox active sites. Nevertheless, current bottlenecks are blamed for their poor intrinsic electronic conductivities, and uncontrollable volume expansion during redox reactions. Given that, an interfacial-confined isochronous conversion strategy is proposed, to prepare orthorhombic/cubic biphasic TMSe heterostructure, namely CuSe/Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub>, through using MXene as the precursor, followed by Cu/Se dual anchorage. As-designed biphasic TMSe heterostructure endows unique hierarchical structure, which contains adequate insertion sites and diffusion spacing for Na ions, besides, the surficial pseudocapacitive storage behaviors can be also proceeded like 2D MXene. By further investigation on electronic structure, the theoretical calculations indicate that biphasic CuSe/Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub> anode exhibits well-enhanced properties, with smaller bandgap and thus greatly improves intrinsic poor conductivities. In addition, the dual redox centers can enhance the electrochemical Na ions storage abilities. As a result, the as-designed biphasic TMSe anode can deliver a reversible specific capacity of 576.8&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, favorable Na affinity, and reduced diffusion barriers. This work discloses a synchronous solution toward demerits in conductivities and lifespan, which is inspiring for TMSe-based anode development in SIBs systems.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202311024-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202311024-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>With the advent of the carbon peaking era and the rapid development of electric vehicles, the current commercial lithium-ion battery (LIB) is difficult to meet the demand for large-scale and sustainable power consumption in the future, due to the increasing shortage of lithium resources and non-uniform distribution issues.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202311024-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202311024-bib-0001_R_d90187490e454" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Energy">1</a>, <a href="#smll202311024-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202311024-bib-0002_R_d90187490e454" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Nat. Mater.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) with a low cost, abundant resources, and similar energy storage mechanisms as LIBs, have been considered as one of the most potential alternatives for next-generation batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202311024-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202311024-bib-0003_R_d90187490e459" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Energy Mater.">3</a>, <a href="#smll202311024-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202311024-bib-0004_R_d90187490e459" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Prog. Mater. Sci.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Owing to the larger ionic radius and sluggish diffusion kinetics of sodium ions (compared to lithium ions), the scalable application of SIBs is limited by the lack of suitable and advantageous host anode materials. Given that, the rational design of the host structure to realize the highly effective Na ion storage is therefore essential to advance the development of next-generation SIBs systems.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202311024-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202311024-bib-0005_R_d90187490e464" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>It differs from Li metal, the highly chemically active Na metal presents serious safety risks under ambient circumstances, which is problematic to be directly used as the anode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202311024-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202311024-bib-0006_R_d90187490e472" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - ACS Nano">6</a>, <a href="#smll202311024-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202311024-bib-0007_R_d90187490e472" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Small">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Including carbon materials,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202311024-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202311024-bib-0008_R_d90187490e477" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Mater.">8</a>-<a href="#smll202311024-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202311024-bib-0010_R_d90187490e477" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Powder Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> alloy materials,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202311024-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202311024-bib-0011_R_d90187490e482" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Mater.">11</a>, <a href="#smll202311024-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202311024-bib-0012_R_d90187490e482" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - ACS Nano">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and transition metal chalcogenides,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202311024-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202311024-bib-0013_R_d90187490e488" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Energy Stor. Mater.">13</a>-<a href="#smll202311024-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202311024-bib-0016_R_d90187490e488" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Powder Mater.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> SIBs anodes are generally categorized as intercalation-, alloy- and conversion type materials, according to reaction mechanism. Amid, transition metal selenides (TMSe) have drawn more research attention (compared to oxides and sulfides counterparts), owing to their metalloid feature, with higher intrinsic conductivity; larger atomic radius, enhanced conversion reaction kinetics resulting from more brittle M─Se bond; and higher theoretical specific capacity. Compared to traditional layered TMSe, like MoSe<sub>2</sub> and WSe<sub>2</sub>, non-layered TMSe endows higher theoretical specific capacities, owing to the multielectron reaction mechanism.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202311024-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202311024-bib-0017_R_d90187490e498" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Energy Mater.">17</a>, <a href="#smll202311024-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202311024-bib-0018_R_d90187490e498" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Energy Stor. Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, this kind of TMSe is mainly found in natural ores, and thus delivers comprehensive merits including cost-effectiveness and simple fabrication, which imparts stronger competitiveness. In general, non-layered TMSe exhibits a relatively lower band gap below 1.0&nbsp;eV, indicating the great potential of the role of SIBs anodes.</p>
         
         <p>However, during the reversible dis-/charging process, the TMSe anode often encounters some challenges.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202311024-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202311024-bib-0019_R_d90187490e506" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Sci.">19</a>-<a href="#smll202311024-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202311024-bib-0023_R_d90187490e506" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Energy Stor. Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> First, based on the conversion reaction mechanism between TMSe and Na ions, the large volume change of active materials will cause the structural collapse of the anode, further resulting in the rapid capacity fade within a limited lifespan. Second, taking carbon-based materials as a reference, the electronic conductivity of TMSe still remains at a lower level, and thus the diffusion kinetics show sluggish features and the rate capabilities are also not satisfying. In the case of the above concerns, the construction of double heterostructure TMSe has been considered a promising strategy to enhance intrinsic electronic conductivity and long-term structural robustness of TMSe anodes, simultaneously. Lately, layered 2D transition metal carbides (MXene), represented by Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>, have become an indispensable member in advancing the rapid development of 2D materials, owing to their unique physical and chemical properties. Most importantly, the 2D nanoflake-like structure of MXene holds great potential in terms of serving as building blocks for heterostructure construction, as well as the rich terminational groups of MXene can also deliver multiple possibilities for design on TMSe-based heterostructure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202311024-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202311024-bib-0024_R_d90187490e519" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Prog. Mater. Sci.">24</a>-<a href="#smll202311024-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202311024-bib-0028_R_d90187490e519" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - J. Energy Chem.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Here, we take V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub> MXene as a 2D layered building block, to realize the synergistic conversion to a biphasic CuSe@Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub> heterostructure on carbon matrix, by virtue of Cu/Se dual anchorage routine. The external Cu and Se species were anchored consecutively on V<sub>2</sub>CT<i><sub>x</sub></i> <i>via</i> electrostatic adsorption and vapor chemical deposition, followed by an isochronous conversion. This biphasic TMSe-based anode exhibits comprehensive merits including metallic conductivities with small band gap, rapid adsorption behaviors on surfaces, multiple active centers for redox reactions, and limited volume expansion from carbon support. As a result, the CuSe /Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub>/C hybrid anode shows excellent rate capability with a charge rate of up to 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and long-term stability over 2,500 cycles. This work shows that the interfacial confinement in-situ conversion routine enables the multi-phase TMSe heterostructure synthesis, to further promote the development of anodes for SIBs systems.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202311024-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202311024-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The interfacial-confined isochronous conversion process was illustrated in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202311024-fig-0001">1a</a>, metallic 2D V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub>x</sub> MXene was used as precursors and priorly adsorbed onto the surface of fungus by feat of H-bond generations. After subsequent electrostatic forces driven Cu<sup>2+</sup> anchorage and Se species gas-phase deposition, the V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub> nanosheets were converted to s sulvanite Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub>, meanwhile, the excessive Cu<sup>2+</sup> directly bind with Se to generate CuSe nanoparticles outside the Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub> layers. Thus, the heterostructure of CuSe/Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub> was formed. The initial step involved the analysis of the physical phase information and chemical composition of the as-designed biphasic TMSe materials, by virtue of X-ray Powder Diffraction (XRD). As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202311024-fig-0001">1b</a>, the predominant characteristic crystal planes of {110}, {111}, and {220} of sulvanite Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub> with a cubic phase nature, could be well-indexed by three intensive diffraction peaks at 18.5°, 32.3°, and 54.0°, respectively, according to the ICSD standard No. JCPDS 62–9148. For other tagged recognizable diffraction peaks, the sharp peaks at 30.8°, 32.5°, 36.3°, 53.1°, and 58.7° belong to the {111}, {112}, {006}, {130}, and {118} lattice facet of CuSe, respectively, which endows orthorhombic phase feature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202311024-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202311024-bib-0029_R_d90187490e599" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Energy Mater.">29</a>, <a href="#smll202311024-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202311024-bib-0030_R_d90187490e599" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Energy Stor. Mater.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Besides, there are no characteristic peaks of V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub> MXene nanosheets in hybrids. The XRD results show the complete conversion from V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub>x</sub> MXene nanosheets to biphasic TMSe of CuSe@Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub> heterostructure, and the diffraction peak curve bread illustrates the amorphous carbon matrix. Besides, the XRD patterns of V<sub>2</sub>AlC MAX and exfoliated V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub>x</sub> MXene nanosheets are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). Then, the micro-morphology was detected to investigate the structural merits of as-designed biphasic TMSe materials. First, the scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images of multilayered V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub>x</sub> nanosheets, <i>Aspergillus Niger</i> fungu precursors, and V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub>x</sub> coated hybrid fibers are illustrated in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2a–c</a> (Supporting Information). After effective exfoliation operation, the mono/few-layered V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub>x</sub> nanosheets could be adsorbed on the smooth surface of fungu fibers, to form a stacking pattern by virtue of H-bond interaction between terminational groups between MXene and chitin.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202311024-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202311024-bib-0031_R_d90187490e646" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - ACS Nano">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> When Cu<sup>2+</sup> ions were introduced, the electrostatic force between them and negatively charged MXene nanosheets played a significant role, which could be confirmed by the Zeta potentials test (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). The strong electrostatic repulsion between V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub>x</sub> MXene nanosheets (−18.2&nbsp;mV) in colloidal solution, enabled outstanding dispersion and stability of few-layered colloidal solution.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202311024-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202311024-bib-0032_R_d90187490e661" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Nano-Micro Lett.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Subsequently, the Se anchorage and isochronous conversion proceeded under annealing treatment, as a result, the interfacial-confined conversion to biphasic TMSe-based anode from V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub>x</sub> nanosheets was achieved. The SEM image of CuSe/Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub>/C is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202311024-fig-0001">1c</a>. Obviously, the nanoflake-like V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub><i>x</i></sub> building blocks were converted to Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub> with a nano-sphere morphology coating on the carbonaceous fibers. The secondary CuSe micro-spheres are deposited on the surface of Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub> layer, owing to the excessive anchorage of Cu and Se species. As multiple hybrids, the mass contents of specific ingredients could be analyzed according to the EDX data, which could be obtained from Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information). Benefiting from this as-formed hierarchical structure, the biphasic TMSe materials endow comprehensive advantages including abundant electrochemically active sites on the surface and rich diffusion pathways for Na ions. In addition, the in situ nitrogen-doped microbial fungus-derived carbon matrix ensured rapid electron transport and enhanced the structural robustness at the same time.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202311024-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202311024-bib-0033_R_d90187490e695" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - ACS Nano">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The 2D nanosheet structure of V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub>x</sub> MXene was sacrificed though, the derived CuSe/Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub> coating holds great potential to reach higher electrochemical activity, which can be more rewarding to the storage of Na<sup>+</sup>.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202311024-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9e9cd16a-62df-4681-b51e-9c62db67677f/smll202311024-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/9e9cd16a-62df-4681-b51e-9c62db67677f/smll202311024-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4edb3651-00b0-401d-9385-c6f2d13c560d/smll202311024-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9e9cd16a-62df-4681-b51e-9c62db67677f/smll202311024-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202311024-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202311024" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Morphological analysis. a) Schematic illustration for as-designed CuSe/Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub>/C. b) XRD patterns for CuSe/Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub>/C samples; c–e) SEM, TEM, and HRTEM images of V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub>x</sub>/C and CuSe/Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub>/C samples; f) elemental mapping images for CuSe/Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub>/C samples.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Detailed lattice information of the CuSe/Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub>/C materials can be found in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202311024-fig-0001">1d,e</a>. Corresponding high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) images exhibit oblivious lattice fringes with d-spacing of 3.21 and 2.0&nbsp;Å, which refers to the {111} and {130} plane of cubic Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub> and orthorhombic CuSe, respectively, which are consistent with XRD analysis above. Furthermore, the hierarchical structure of the as-design biphasic TMSe heterostructure could be further indicated, considering the obvious deposition state of CuSe particles on the Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub> coating. From the perspective of elemental distribution, the individual mapping images for V, Cu, Se, C, and N of Cu<sub>x</sub>Se/Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub>/C hybrids are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202311024-fig-0001">1f</a>. It is evident that the V element was only presented in the substance of V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub>x</sub> MXene derived Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub> nanospheres layer, while Cu and Se elements were concentrated in the entire biphasic TMSe heterostructure including the deposited particles, demonstrating distinctive hierarchical structural features as in the aforementioned SEM and TEM discussion.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202311024-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202311024-bib-0034_R_d90187490e789" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Adv. Mater.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, the inner carbonaceous matrix was mainly dominated by C and N elements, serving as a robust skeleton.</p>
         
         <p>Following textural properties investigation, Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) nitrogen adsorption/desorption test was conducted to further evaluate the specific surface area and pore size distribution of both V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub>x</sub>/C and biphasic CuSe/Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub>/C materials. As the result shows in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202311024-fig-0002">2a</a>, the typical H3 adsorption curve generally corresponded to a number of narrow pores formed by the accumulation of sheet-like particles. It is concluded that the characteristics of the composite hierarchical structure are consistent with the SEM results above. Compared to pure V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub>x</sub> precursor hybrid, the specific surface area of CuSe/Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub>/C composite is increased from 10.26 to 34.93&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, suggesting the enriched adsorption sites for Na ions after as- proposed interfacial-confined isochronous conversion routine. Meanwhile, the pore size distributions are also shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202311024-fig-0002">2b</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information), the shared mesoporous structure was manifested, which is also beneficial for Na ions diffusion. Due to the changes in the morphology for the formation of CuSe/Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub> heterostructure, the average pore size decreased while the specific surface area increased, from 4.85 to 5.94&nbsp;nm specifically, which were beneficial to improve the infiltration of electrolytes and further facilitate the rapid insertion process. Meanwhile, from the perspective of pore volume variation, the pore volume of as-designed CuSe/Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub>/C hybrids shows an apparent increased value to a twofold higher (0.037&nbsp;cm<sup>3</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) than that of pristine V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub>x</sub> hybrids (0.015&nbsp;cm<sup>3</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>). This kind of enhancement in terms of pore volume imparts great potential for accommodating adequate sodium ions during SIBs operations.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202311024-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/11bdb217-1910-4681-8848-969637519452/smll202311024-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/11bdb217-1910-4681-8848-969637519452/smll202311024-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e61b124e-c596-4fe8-a01a-2522e0e754cf/smll202311024-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/11bdb217-1910-4681-8848-969637519452/smll202311024-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202311024-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202311024" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Structural analysis. a) BET patterns of V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub>x</sub>/C and CuSe/Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub>/C samples; b) Pose-size distribution result of CuSe/Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub>/C samples; c) TG-DSC curves for V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub>x</sub>/C and CuSe/Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub>/C samples; d–f) high-resolution XPS spectra of Cu 2<i>p</i>, V <i>2p</i>, and Se 3<i>d</i> in CuSe/Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub>/C samples.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The thermal stability is also a valuable property for materials investigation, thus the CuSe/Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub>/C hybrid was analyzed by the Thermogravimetric-Differential scanning calorimetry&nbsp;(TG-DSC) technique. As recorded in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202311024-fig-0002">2c</a>, apparently, there are two weight loss stages during consecutive rise in temperature, from ambient to 1,000&nbsp;°C. The first stage at ≈400&nbsp;°C, with an intensive exothermic peak, should be attributed to the oxidation of CuSe/Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub> heterostructure, to obtain vanadium and cooper oxides due to the air atmosphere condition. Meanwhile, Se species would turn to SeO<sub>2</sub> followed by its sublimation under this temperature, and thus the weight loss is up to ≈60%. Subsequently, when environmental temperature rises to higher than 400&nbsp;°C, the carbon matrix begins to undergo the combustion process, the final product is supposed to mainly include the vanadium and copper oxides.</p>
         
         <p>To further investigate the chemical states of the biphasic TMSe materials, the high-resolution X-ray Photoelectron Spectroscopy (XPS) spectra of Cu 2<i>p</i>, V 2<i>p</i>, and Se 3<i>d</i> are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202311024-fig-0002">2d–f</a>. For CuSe/ Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub> heterostructure, both Cu and V could see two couples of separated fitting peaks for 2<i>p</i> orbitals, which means the rich valence states inside. In detail, the two predominant peaks in the core-level spectrum of Cu 2<i>p</i> could be classified into Cu 2<i>p<sub>3/2</sub></i> and Cu 2<i>p<sub>1/2</sub></i> with the binding energy of 933.2 and 953.1&nbsp;eV, manifesting the co-existence of both Cu<sup>+</sup> and Cu<sup>2+</sup> ions. And the species states of V element mainly include V<sup>4+</sup> and V<sup>5+</sup>, which could be concluded based on the V 2<i>p<sub>3/2</sub></i> and V 2<i>p<sub>1/2</sub></i> orbitals with binding energies ≈516 and 523&nbsp;eV, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202311024-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202311024-bib-0034_R_d90187490e970" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Adv. Mater.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The abundance of valence states in heterostructure could deliver multiple active centers for redox reactions, compared to sing-phase TMSe, further boosting the electrochemical Na ion storage performance. In Se 3<i>d</i> spectrum, two specific signals could be fitted, which centered at 54.2 and 55.9&nbsp;eV, assigning to the electronic orbits of 3<i>d</i><sub>5/2</sub> and 3<i>d</i><sub>3/2</sub> of Se<sup>2−</sup>, the higher binding energy owing to the heterostructure nature of selenides. Then, the fungu-derived carbon matrix was further investigated by the XPS technique. In Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6a</a> (Supporting Information), the fitting peaks of C 1<i>s</i> orbitals at the binding energies of 284.4, 285.1, 286.3, and 288.7&nbsp;eV, are in corresponding to C─C/C═C, C─N, C─O, and C═N bonds, indicating the presence of N-doped heteroatoms in the carbon matrix.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202311024-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202311024-bib-0035_R_d90187490e991" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Consequently, the absence of the peak at the binding energy of 282.3&nbsp;eV implies the breaking of the C─V bond. It is believed that the V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub>x</sub> MXene was converted to Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub> completely during the isochronous conversion process. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6b</a> (Supporting Information), the three types N doped states were graphitized nitrogen, pyrrolic nitrogen, and pyridine nitrogen, which can be found in the peaks situated at 398.4, 399.7, and 400.8&nbsp;eV in the high-resolution XPS spectra of N 1<i>s</i>. This in situ nitrogen doping effect was beneficial in improving the electronic conductivity of the carbonaceous matrix. In addition, the peak at 399.1&nbsp;eV corresponds to the bond of Se-N, indicating that external Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub> particles as electrochemical active units of coating layers on carbon matrix, show great potential for structural robustness during reversible battery operations. Raman spectroscopy was adopted to detect the atomic structure on the surface of heterostructures and the graphite degree of the carbon matrix. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> (Supporting Information), the two activity peaks located at 289 and 409&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> were matched with in-plane bending vibration (<i>E</i><sub>g</sub>) and out-of-plane bending vibration (A<sub>1g</sub>) of vanadium atom, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202311024-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202311024-bib-0036_R_d90187490e1026" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Energy Stor. Mater.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Meanwhile, the non-negligible peak at ≈260&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> could be indexed to the phonon mode of the first longitudinal optic Cu-Se vibrations. As for V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub>x</sub>/C hybrids, the peaks in the spectrum in the low wavenumber range could be attributed to V─O vibration, which is caused by mild surficial oxidation of MXenes. Furthermore, the characteristic peaks positioned at 1379.06 and 1381.59&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> were correlated to defective carbon and graphitic carbon of the carbon matrix. It can be referred to the graphitization and defect degree with the ratio of them (I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202311024-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202311024-bib-0037_R_d90187490e1044" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Adv. Mater.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As a result, the value of I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> was 0.83 for the heterostructure, which was larger than that of carbon matrix material coated with V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub>x</sub> MXene (0.75). Clearly, the CuSe/Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub>/C materials endow a higher proportion of defects after high-temperature treatment. The high disorder degree of the carbon matrix is attributed to the presence of doped atomic nitrogen so that more active sites for Na<sup>+</sup> are provided and the electrochemical performance can be further optimized, which can be demonstrated in subsequent electrochemical performance evaluations.</p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">Except for the above XPS analysis, the X-ray auger electron spectroscopy (AES) was conducted to further identify the chemical activity of Cu elements in the as-designed CuSe/Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub> heterostructure. As demonstrated in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202311024-fig-0003">3a</a>, the fitting peaks at the auger kinetic energies of 917.9 and 916.1&nbsp;eV corresponded to Cu<sup>2+</sup> and Cu<sup>+</sup>, respectively, further indicating the polyvalent state of Cu in the heterostructure, in this way, the biphasic TMSe-based heterostructure holds great potential in terms of enhanced electrochemical properties.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202311024-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202311024-bib-0038_R_d90187490e1082" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To verify this point of view, as-prepared CuSe/Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub>/C materials serve as an anode in a coin-cell battery, with Na foil as counter and reference electrodes. First, the cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves within the voltage range from 0.01 to 3.0&nbsp;V are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202311024-fig-0003">3b</a>, including the initial three cycles at a scanning rate of 0.2&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>. In the first cycle, the irreversible cathodic peak ≈0.78&nbsp;V could be ascribed to the phase transition of initial intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> on the surficial sites of CuSe/Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub> heterostructure, as well as the formation of solid electrolyte interface (SEI). According to the above structural analysis, the beneficial hierarchical biphasic TMSe structure could provide adequate adsorption sites for the intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup>, and the excessive Na ions from the electrolyte would tend to diffuse into the internal carbon matrix, therefore, the voltage would change rapidly at a low potential ≈0.01&nbsp;V, which was also a typical characteristic of sodium-ion storage behavior of carbon-based anode. The coincident redox peak in the following second and third cycles illustrated that the Na ions insertion process into CuSe/Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub> heterostructure was highly reversible during continuous dis-/charging reactions. Among other redox peaks in CV curves, the reduction peaks at ≈0.6 and 1.2&nbsp;V might belong to the alloyed Na<sub>2</sub>Se, as well as the elements Cu and V. In the anodic sweep, the reversible oxidation peaks ≈1.5 and 1.8&nbsp;V correspond to the extraction reaction of Na<sup>+</sup> and regeneration of CuSe/Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub> heterostructure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202311024-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202311024-bib-0039_R_d90187490e1119" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Adv. Energy Mater.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The reaction can be written as follows:

         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202311024-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d8b66f13-dace-4804-8b79-c6f557ee10e7/smll202311024-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/d8b66f13-dace-4804-8b79-c6f557ee10e7/smll202311024-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b94d23ed-6eee-45fd-bb09-e134ef853d70/smll202311024-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d8b66f13-dace-4804-8b79-c6f557ee10e7/smll202311024-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202311024-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202311024" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical evaluation. a) AES profiles of Cu element in CuSe/Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub>/C sample. b) Initial CV profiles at 0.2&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> of first three cycles for CuSe/Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub>/C; c,d) Electrochemical cyclic performance for V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub>x</sub>/C and CuSe/Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub>/C samples at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> over 150 cycles and 1.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> over 2500 cycles, respectively; e) rate capability of V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub>x</sub>/C and CuSe/Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub>/C samples; f,g) Capacitive contribution ratios of V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub>x</sub>/C and CuSe/Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub>/C samples at various scan rates; h) Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient during dis-/charging process.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8a</a> (Supporting Information) shows the CV curves of V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub>x</sub>/C electrode at a various scan rate from 0.2 to 1.0&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>. The symmetric redox peaks in consecutive CV curves belonged to the insertion and extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> on the surface of V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub>x</sub> MXene, with a trait of non-diffusion pseudocapacitive behaviors, which is consistent with previous research results. Subsequently, To further evaluate the electrochemical properties of as-prepared biphasic TMSe anodes, the long-term dis-/charging cyclic properties at constant and dynamic current densities were performed. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202311024-fig-0003">3c</a>, the initial dis-/charging capacities of biphasic CuSe/Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub>/C anode were 638.5 and 1022.9&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, and the initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) is only 62.4%, which were related to the SEI film formed on the electrode surface during the initial intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> and well consistent with the CV analysis. As for V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub>x</sub>/C electrode, the initial charge-discharge specific capacities were 372.2/689.5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, and the corresponding ICE was 53.9%. The difference in ICEs between them is mainly because of the structural merits of biphasic CuSe/Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub> heterostructure with carbon. In detail, the hierarchical mesoporous structure of biphasic TMSe anode could deliver adequate electrochemical active sites for Na ions adsorption and diffusion. in the initial process. As a result, a large amount of sodium storage sites could be fully exploited after initial several cycles. After 150 cycles, the specific capacity can reach 576.8&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, resulting in a Coulombic efficiency close to 100%. As a comparison, the sodium storage capacity of V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub>x</sub>/C was only 281.9&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> in the absence of biphasic CuSe/Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub> transformation on the carbon matrix. Notably, CuSe/Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub>/C endows excellent cyclic stability with improved electrochemical sodium storage capacity, demonstrating the advancement of as-designed unique hierarchical mesoporous biphasic TMSe materials. In addition, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202311024-fig-0003">3d</a> displays the cyclic robustness at ultrahigh current densities of 10.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. After 2500 charge-discharge cycles, the reversible sodium storage capacities of CuSe/Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub>/C and V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub>x</sub>/C anodes could still exhibit a high specific capacity of 460.1 and 237.4&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The average capacity loss rate was only 0.0021% per cycle according to the corresponding initial capacity, and the Coulombic efficiencies can hold as high as 99.9% and 99.7%, respectively, here again, as-prepared biphasic TMSe anode shows much better cyclic robustness than traditional single-phase TMSe anode. As for the situation under dynamic conditions, the electrochemical properties of both anodes at different current densities are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202311024-fig-0003">3e</a>. When the charge-discharge rate increased from 0.1 to 10.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, the average discharge-specific capacities were 621.6, 561.9, 504.1, 460.2, 412.5, 384.3, and 330.8&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Then, at the condition of the current density's recovery to 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, the reversible sodium storage capacity could be maintained at 543.6&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, as-designed biphasic TMSe anode shows satisfying rate capabilities, which could be attributed to the following reasons: i) similar to 2D MXene-based anode, the surface pseudocapacitive behaviors hold rapid diffusion and adsorption advantages compared to other counterparts, ii) dual electrochemical active sites of both Cu and V could exhibit higher sodium ions storage abilities, iii) N-doped carbonaceous support could well confine the volume expansion of single-phase TMSe anodes, further guarantee the good structural robustness, iv) last but not least, the electronic conductivities of as-prepared biphasic TMSe heterostructure could be well enhanced, which would be further discussed based on the DFT calculations in following section. It is worth mentioning that, the as-designed biphasic heterostructure could be well-maintained after long-term cyclic operations, only with the formation of SEI interfaces (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). As-designed biphasic heterostructure could be well-maintained after long-term cyclic operations, only with the formation of SEI interfaces.</p>
         
         <p>In terms of Na ions diffusion kinetic properties, the detailed analysis was conducted based on the CV curves at different sweep rates of biphasic CuSe/Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub>/C anode were conducted. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10a</a> (Supporting Information) shows the integrated patterns of CV curves for CuSe/Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub>/C anode, with the changing sweep rates of 0.2, 0.4, 0.6, 0.8, and 1.0&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>. Clearly, with the increase of the sweep rates, the area of the pattern enclosed by the CV curve gradually grew, while the outline of curves remained unchanged, which manifests that, the enhanced electronic conductivities and the structural robustness during the dynamic discharging process. By plotting the logarithmic relationship between peak current and potential: <i>i</i> = a<i>v</i><sup>b</sup> ↔ log(<i>i</i>) = b log(<i>v</i>) + log(a), different types of capacitance contribution proportions could be qualitatively analyzed. Here, as the <i>b</i> is close to the value of 1.0, the Na ions storage behaviors could be identified as surface pseudocapacitive domination, while when it is getting closer to 0.5 represents a diffusion-controlled mechanism. As depicted in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10b</a> (Supporting Information), the b values of two pairs of redox peaks were 0.98/1.0 and 0.71/0.60, respectively. Accordingly, it is indicated that, the hybrid sodium storage mechanism of as-designed biphasic CuSe/Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub>/C heterostructure. Namely, the synergistic effect of diffusion control and surface pseudocapacitive behavior ensured the high-level sodium storage capacity and rate performance. To further confirm capacity contribution ratios of individual energy storage behaviors at different scanning rates, the separation of current can be quantified following the equation: <i>i</i> = k<sub>1</sub><i>v</i> + k<sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup>. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202311024-fig-0003">3f</a>, when the scan rate increased to 1.0&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>, the surface pseudocapacitive contribution proportion of biphasic CuSe/Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub> TMSe-based anode can reach 84.7%, and the corresponding diffusion-controlled contribution proportion was 15.3%, demonstrating the biphasic TMSe heterostructure is mainly adopted rapid surface pseudocapacitive behavior to finish the sodium ion storage at high sweep rates. With the increase in scan rate, the contribution ratio of pseudocapacitive contributions gradually raised, which were 58.9%, 62.3%, 71.2%, and 78.9%, respectively. Similarly, for V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub>x</sub>/C sample, the pseudocapacitive contributions share the same variation tendency, the value of ratios increased from 55.1%, 68.6%, 72.2%, and 81.4% to 88.2%, corresponding to sweep rates varied from 0.2 to 1.0&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>, respectively.</p>
         
         <p>Similar to the above analysis, the sodium-ion storage kinetics of V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub>x</sub>/C anode material was also analyzed. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8a</a> (Supporting Information) shows CV profiles at different sweep rates of MXene-coated anodes. It can be seen that the redox peak at ≈0.6&nbsp;V corresponds to the adsorption of Na ions onto the surface of V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub>x</sub> MXene. Then, the redox peaks become more obvious with scan rates increase, which indicates that the fungus-derived carbon matrix coated by V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub>x</sub> MXene nanosheet could deliver better electrochemical performance at higher rates. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8b</a> (Supporting Information), the <i>b</i> value calculated following the above-mentioned formula was ≈0.93, indicating a pseudocapacitive-dominating storage mechanism during the intercalation/deintercalation of sodium ions due to the uniform coating of external V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub>x</sub> MXene nanosheets and the synergistic interaction of internal fungus-derived carbon in V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub>x</sub>/C anode. At the sweep rate of 1.0&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>, the pseudocapacitive contribution ratio could approach 88.2% (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202311024-fig-0003">3g</a>). Compared to biphasic CuSe/Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub> TMSe-based heterostructure, the V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub>x</sub>/C also shows a pseudocapacitive domination Na ions storage mechanism, which retains the surface adsorption advantages of rapid storage mechanism for V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub>x</sub> MXene, accompanied by inner carbon matrix support. Besides, the pseudocapacitive contributions could reach 55.1%, 68.6%, 72.2%, 81.4%, and 84.7%, since the scan rate was increased from 0.2 to 1.0&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>, demonstrating that the anchoraged CuSe and V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub>x</sub>-derived Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub> endowed hierarchical structure with abundant electrochemical active storage sites after gas phase selenization, contributing to further promotion of the sodium storage performance. Besides, the reaction kinetics at the situation of before and after dis-/charge cycles were investigated, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a> (Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <p>Then, the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) was used to evaluate the sodium-ion diffusion ability of the biphasic CuSe/Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub>/C hierarchical structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202311024-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202311024-bib-0036_R_d90187490e1520" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Energy Stor. Mater.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a> (Supporting Information) shows the GITT curve at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with a resting time of 4 h and a relaxation time of 8 min. Numerically, the sodium ion diffusion coefficient (D<sub>Na</sub>) can be calculated according to Fick's second law: D<sub>Na</sub> = 4/πτ (m<sub>B</sub>V<sub>m</sub>δE<sub>s</sub>)<sup>2</sup>(M<sub>b</sub>SδE<sub>τ</sub>)<sup>−1</sup>. As the result shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202311024-fig-0003">3h</a>, the order of magnitude of D<sub>Na</sub> was ≈10<sup>−6</sup>–10<sup>−10</sup>&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> during sodium intercalation/deintercalation, which outperformed some reported MXene-based anodes These results demonstrated that the hierarchical CuSe/Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub>/C biphasic TMSe anode is beneficial to achieve rapid sodium ion storage, which is mainly resulted from the enhanced electronic conductivities owing to the heterostructure formation, as well as the strengthened structural robustness. Besides, the in situ N-doped effect inside the carbon matrix could also help improve the conductivities and further Na ions storage sites, as a result, the electrochemical performance could be well-enhanced under the synergistic structure–property design.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202311024-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202311024-bib-0040_R_d90187490e1569" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Prog. Mater. Sci.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>As above discussed, the design of biphasic CuSe/Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub> heterostructure endows numerous merits for serving as an advanced anode for SIBs, especially when compared with similar Cu-based selenides anodes (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). In theory, the conductivities, adsorption, and diffusion properties could be well simulated by DFT calculations, and thus, provide valid proof for our point of view. As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202311024-fig-0004">4a–c</a>, localized densities of states (LDOS) results of V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub>x</sub>/C and CuSe/Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub>/C materials. For V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub>x</sub> MXene coated hybrids, the electronic structure shows metallic electronic conductivities, which inherited the advantages of MXene materials. After the interfacial-confined isochronous conversion from V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub>x</sub> MXene, the biphasic CuSe/Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub> TMSe-based materials also show a rich state of electrons around Fermi level, which means the biphasic heterostructure can deliver satisfying electronic conductivities, indeed. In other words, the band gap (0.82&nbsp;eV) of sulvanite Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub> could be well reduced by virtue of biphasic heterostructure construction, and one of the main challenges for bimetallic TMSe anodes (poor electronic conductivities) could be addressed as well. As LDOS results show, the intrinsic physical properties could be regulated under the condition of biphasic TMSe heterostructure construction. Subsequently, the adsorption and diffusion behaviors could be regarded as direct proof to evaluate the structure-property relationships. In terms of Na adsorption, the optimized adsorption model for both anodes is illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202311024-fig-0004">4d</a> and Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>–<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a> (Supporting Information), to highlight the hierarchical structural traits of biphasic TMSe heterostructure, the Na species were released on the different sites on the surface. Correspondingly, the adsorption energies for different sites were summarized in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202311024-fig-0004">4e</a>, compared to the highly conductive V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub>x</sub> MXene sample, the as-designed biphasic TMSe heterostructure shows more beneficial adsorption affinities toward Na species, which could be regarded as a main reason for better electrochemical behaviors for CuSe/Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub> anode. Besides, as the diffusion pathways simulations illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202311024-fig-0004">4f,g</a>, the biphasic CuSe/Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub> heterostructure could deliver reduced diffusion barriers during the Na ions diffusion process, which could be attributed to the enhanced electronic conductivities together with the stronger affinities. All in all, the as-designed biphasic TMSe heterostructure was converted from metallic-like MXene, holding the inherent high conductivity with small band gap, and the multiple redox centers could also provide abundant active sites for Na ions. Consequently, the well-enhanced electrochemical properties of biphasic selenide anodes could steadily operate in an extended lifespan is also ascribed to the carbonaceous matrix as a support to alleviate the volume expansion of TMSe materials (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202311024-fig-0005">5</a>), which further highlighted the superiority compared to other SIBs anodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202311024-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202311024-bib-0041_R_d90187490e1650" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Acta Phys.-Chim. Sin.">41</a>-<a href="#smll202311024-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202311024-bib-0043_R_d90187490e1650" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - InfoMat">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202311024-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e7df1f8d-c995-494b-8329-ba0374236bc5/smll202311024-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/e7df1f8d-c995-494b-8329-ba0374236bc5/smll202311024-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/5d1527f2-d6f4-453b-9213-d5792024e908/smll202311024-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e7df1f8d-c995-494b-8329-ba0374236bc5/smll202311024-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">

         </section>

      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202311024-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202311024-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, highly conductive V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub>x</sub> MXene and microbial fungi were used as co-precursors to prepare a biphasic transition metal selenides heterostructure, by feat of interfacial-confined isochronous conversion routine. As-prepared biphasic TMSe anodes exhibit a hierarchical structure that endows abundant active site sites, enhanced electronic conductivities, and multiple redox centers for high-performance Na ion storage. Additionally, the in situ N-doped carbonaceous matrix as a structural skeleton could well confine the volume expansion of CuSe/Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub> heterostructure, further prolonging the lifespan in SIBs. During the synthesis process, the excessive pre-anchorage Cu ions on V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub>x</sub> MXene can boost the formation of CuSe/Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub> heterostructure, because of the complete conversion of Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub> from V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub>x</sub> MXene. As a result, the biphasic CuSe/Cu<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub> anode exhibits excellent rate capabilities, with 330.8&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> when current density is up to 10.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, and the stable Na ion storage at high rates over 2500 cycles. Compared to carbon-based SIBs anodes, this biphasic TMSe anode can deliver comparable electronic conductivities and cyclic lifespan with unparalleled electrochemical sodium ion storage abilities, especially at higher rates. This work has enlightened a new design strategy for biphasic heterostructure design of TMSe-based materials, taking 2D-layered MXene materials as precursors.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
